0060558121|4.0|Although I gave this book \\"only\\" 4 stars, in reality I wish Amazon had a feature so I could give it 4.5, which is what I feel it truly deserves.<br /><br />American Gods is among the longest of Neil Gaiman's novels. Possibly because of this, I found it to be slower paced than Neverwhere, with a slightly meandering storyline in places, including one segment roughly in the middle where the main character, Shadow, is left to live in a small town while his employer goes elsewhere. Despite such blips, American Gods retains the staples of Gaiman's writing. It is funny and imaginative, with great characters and an interesting plot, and what can only be described as excellent writing. It is a very solid novel, enjoyable and engrossing. Compared to Neverwhere and Anansi boys, which I feel are like this novel's cousins, this book is moderately more difficult. Instead of simply following Shadow, the book covers several points of view, and several time periods unrelated to the main plot, but which fill out the details of the background nicely.<br /><br />I think in particular, Gaiman's choice of setting in this book was a good one. The American Midwest isn't a setting commonly seen, at least in the books that I've read, and it gives the book a sense of otherness, while still keeping it firmly in the real world. Compounded by the vast numbers of mythical beings that populate the universe of American Gods, and you get a world that strikes a perfect balance of similarity and the unknown. We can relate to the characters and their situations, and yet still feel curious and mystified.<br /><br />Ultimately, this is a great read. It's entertainment value is sky high, it's exciting, humorous, and once you get going you won't want to put the book down until the end. And at the end of the day, once you've finished the book, the great thing about the world Gaiman has created is that it sticks with you, and you almost wish that such things really did exist in our world too. When a book does that, and causes you to think about other mythology and folklore, and even look them up because you're curious and wish to know more, it becomes more than a bit of fluff read for entertainment value alone. American Gods is a great read, and comes strongly recommended.s that, and causes you to think about other mythology and folklore, and even look them up because you're curious and wish to know more, it becomes more than a bit of fluff read for entertainment value alone. 0060557818|5.0|Neverwhere is in the interesting genre of fantasy that does not occur in a far away alternate universe, or where ancient forces of good and evil clash. Instead, it is set in our world. In London, in particular. With one tiny difference. In Neverwhere, as in many of Gaiman's stories, the legends and folklore are all real, and magical and supernatural beings still exist in the world, trying to make their way in while all around them modern life goes on.<br /><br />Neverwhere is the story of Richard Mayhew, a nice perfectly ordinary man, who unwittingly stumbles into this underground world. His struggle to escape it and return to his former way of living, the things he encounters, the decisions he makes, and the strange adventure he winds up in due to a single charitable act form the meat of plot, and are about the only details I can give without going into spoilers. Gaiman writes with an excellent brand of humor that begins with the very first chapter and does not let go until the novel is over. All in all, this is a light read, but don't think it is just for kids. This is something that anyone can enjoy, and if you are looking for something entertaining, funny, fun, and yet intriguing and interesting, this is a book you should most certainly pick up.

1401233384|1.0|Since 1985-86 and the creation of the \\"Event\\" comic with the advent of [[ASIN:1563897504 Crisis On Infinite Earths]] and [[ASIN:1904159834 Marvel Super Heroes Secret Wars]], comic fans have been inundated yearly with these \\"game-changing, status quo-shaking\\" multi-title crossovers that are supposed to ripple through the universe. To be clear, I haven't been a huge fan of these over the years because they always fall short of what they promise to do. However, occasionally, you do get something that promises change and actually follows through, at least for a year or so. More \\"minor\\" event comics like Marvel's SPIDER-ISLAND, THE DEATH OF SPIDER-MAN or DC's BLACKEST NIGHT/BRIGHTEST DAY books have actually changed things about the characters involved and made the architecture of their respective universes different.<br /><br />FLASHPOINT is NOT one of those comics.<br /><br />Geoff Johns, DC's current Chief Creative Officer, and EIC Dan DiDio saw the sinking revenue of comics, and frightened by a possible return to the dark sales era of the 1990's, decided to make a massive change for the DC Universe. They announced FLASHPOINT as a five-part series that would change the DC Universe forever, and once completed, it would leave the company with 52 major titles (simply dubbed \\"The New 52\\") that were going to reflect the new direction for the characters in the DC Universe, and it would even bring characters who had long been in DC's VERTIGO books into DC's mainstream titles. And with Johns heading up every major event comic over the last several years but one, and that was Grant Morrison's steamy mess of FINAL CRISIS, he decided to take the reins on this event, get superstar artist Andy Kubert, and create a new universe for DC characters to exist in.<br /><br />But it's not the lead-in to The New 52. This is merely another alternate universe that, when all the sound and fury is over, has ABSOLUTELY NOTHING TO DO WITH ANYTHING. Now, not too long ago Johns wrote the very successful (and well-written) GREEN LANTERN: REBIRTH, when he brought Hal Jordan back to life, DC basically gave him carte blanche. He went on to write GREEN LANTERN, and he was very good at that for a while, particularly during THE SINESTRO CORPS WAR, GREEN LANTERN SECRET ORIGIN, and BLACKEST NIGHT, but he also did INFINITE CRISIS, which while messy, wasn't awful. He also decided to do one more character that literally wasn't necessary, and wasn't even really wanted by the fans. He, and the Powers That Be at DC, despite the great sacrifice Barry Allen made for \\"the multiverse\\" way back in CRISIS ON INFINITE EARTHS, and the fans that embraced Wally West as the new Flash, felt that Barry should come back, and hence FLASH: REBIRTH. It's a totally needless resurrection of a beloved character that actually had more resonance being dead rather than alive. And the story of that series was a joke too. I would almost rather spoil it if you haven't read it because it will save you time you'll never get back, but I don't do spoilers. Suffice it to say, the main villain of the tale is the same main villain of FLASHPOINT. And the motivations make as little sense here in FLASHPOINT as they did in REBIRTH.<br /><br />Basically, all this is is an alternate timeline tale starring Barry Allen as he goes back in time to save his mother from her murderer. When he does that, the entire timeline has become a madhouse! IT'S A MAAAAAADHOUSE!!! There's no Superman; The Amazons (Wonder Woman) are at a war with The Atlanteans (Aquaman) that has cost millions of lives; the character Cyborg is apparently the only real hero in this universe, Oliver Queen never got out of the arms business, Abin Sur never died and thus Hal Jordan never became a Green Lantern, and Batman is killing criminals and has successfully turned Gotham City into a huge gambling town. Oh, and also of course, The Flash has never existed. As the series (and its several different tie-ins) continues, The Flash, Batman and Cyborg must team up to save this world. Yes, it's basically the worst possible place to live.<br /><br />What troubles me so much about this tale is that it actually has NO EFFECT on the actual DC Universe other than the merging of some Vertigo titles like HELLBLAZER, ANIMAL MAN and SWAMP THING, as well as WildStorm titles like STORMWATCH and GRIFTER into the main DC Universe. This supposedly kick-starts \\"The New 52\\", but only some of it. Characters like Batman, the Barbara Gordon Batgirl, Batwoman (basically every member of the Bat-Family) are unchanged, as well as Green Lantern. Other characters are changed, but the changes make no sense. Oh, and apparently, the current DC Universe has only taken place over the course of FIVE YEARS. One way to point out how incredibly stupid that is is that Batman has had five different Robins in five years, since they all exist in this universe (with the exception of Stephanie Brown, who despite having a very successful and very good solo comic as Batgirl, they didn't feel it was necessary to include her... which was a pretty sucky thing to do). Just think about that for as long as it takes to read this sentence. I'm sure it's clear to you as it is to me that the \\"five year\\" idea is amazingly moronic, but people who make lots of money and are supposed to be really smart and creative made this idea up, probably over a few weeks of meetings and then committed to it (which you have to admit takes some brass). Anyway, I digress from this actual book.<br /><br />Andy Kubert is an extraordinarily talented artist, but sadly his work is hamstrung by the deadlines this comic had to make sure it could get every little thing down in order to make thr projected date of the launch of The New 52. Frankly, the series is nothing more than a cash-grab and what's worse is that it's written like one. Johns is a very talented writer; don't get me wrong. I really like some of the stuff he's done. He seems to put a lot of time into everything, even if it's not terribly good. FLASHPOINT is so far in the wrong direction, and it doesn't even seem like it was something that any real thought or planning went into when you read it, despite the fact that all of the many tie-in series must have taken a great deal of planning and developing and writing and drawing. I hate to pun like this, but FLASHPOINT as an event comic is all flash and no substance.<br /><br />Aside from a few \\"Whoa!\\" moments, FLASHPOINT falls flat on its face from a storytelling perspective. It's a mish-mash of hackneyed ideas, lame characters, pointless motivation and a tremendously lackluster ending.<br /><br />This is not to say that everything about the FLASHPOINT event was bad, because it did yield two very good tie-in series; BATMAN: KNIGHT OF VENGEANCE from Brian Azzarello and Eduardo Rizzo, which can be found in [[ASIN:1401234054 Flashpoint: The World of Flashpoint Featuring Batman]], and PROJECT: SUPERMAN, a series over-seen by DC's very best writer, Scott Snyder, and done by Lowell Francis and Gene Ha found in [[ASIN:1401234348 Flashpoint: The World of Flashpoint Featuring Superman]]. These were two excellent series borne out of a steaming hot mess, which makes FLASHPOINT the exception to the rule that necessity is the mother of invention.<br /><br />At least when you go back in time to save that mother and end up creating a really, really awful alternate timeline.t team up to save this world. Yes, it's basically the worst possible place to live.<br /><br />What troubles me so much about this tale is that it actually has NO EFFECT on the actual DC Universe other than the merging of some Vertigo titles like HELLBLAZER, ANIMAL MAN and SWAMP THING, as well as WildStorm titles like STORMWATCH and GRIFTER into the main DC Universe. This supposedly kick-starts \\"The New 52\\", but only some of it. Characters like Batman, the Barbara Gordon Batgirl, Batwoman (basically every member of the Bat-Family) are unchanged, as well as Green Lantern. Other characters are changed, but the changes make no sense. Oh, and apparently, the current DC Universe has only taken place over the course of FIVE YEARS. One way to point out how incredibly stupid that is is that Batman has had five different Robins in five years, since they all exist in this universe (with the exception of Stephanie Brown, who despite having a very successful and very good solo comic as Batgirl, they didn't feel it was necessary to include her... which was a pretty sucky thing to do). Just think about that for as long as it takes to read this sentence. I'm sure it's clear to you as it is to me that the \\"five year\\" idea is amazingly moronic, but people who make lots of money and are supposed to be really smart and creative made this idea up, probably over a few weeks of meetings and then committed to it (which you have to admit takes some brass). Anyway, I digress from this actual book.<br /><br />Andy Kubert is an extraordinarily talented artist, but sadly his work is hamstrung by the deadlines this comic had to make sure it could get every little thing down in order to make thr projected date of the launch of The New 52. Frankly, the series is nothing more than a cash-grab and what's worse is that it's written like one. Johns is a very talented writer; don't get me wrong. I really like some of the stuff he's done. He seems to put a lot of time into everything, even if it's notterribly good. B002XTBE6K|4.0|I loved this version and intensely disliked it at the same time.  Most of the cast was absolutely perfect and really brought their characters to life.  Michael Gambon was especially delightful as Mr. Woodhouse (\\"in case the dancers should do something remarkably foolish...like opening a window\\") - lovable, charming, and brilliantly deadpan. Blake Riston was similarly humorous as the fatuous Mr. Elton.<br /><br />Austen is tricky to adapt because of her strong narrative voice.  Extensive narration isn't an effective tool in film, so adaptations usually struggle with replicating her wonderful dry wit, but this one does it better than most. The minor characters manage to convey the jokes Austen made at their expense and let the audience in on the joke, not an easy task. Mr. Woodhouse's neuroses, Mr. Elton's vanity and foolishness, Harriet's paltry brain activity etc are portrayed in such a way that you get some of Austen's humor, if not her voice.  What is remarkable about this version of Emma is that it's populated by Austen's actual characters, not signifiers for those characters.  Most adaptations are a window onto the world of the book - you get a partial version of Mr. Knightley, or Mr. Bingley, or whoever, and the film relies on you, the reader/viewer to fill in the details from the book.  This Emma takes no such shortcut - it truly brings the book alive.  Well, the supporting characters.<br /><br />Where it falls completely short is in the title character herself.  I think the script, direction, and actress are equally to blame, but whatever it is, it's maddening.  Romola Garai is imitating another famous RG, Regina George, and starring in Emma: Mean Girls edition.  She's loud (what is with the shrieking?), shallow (Emma is supposed to be intelligent), has exaggerated bratty teenager mannerisms (\\"um, no\\"?!?! Are we in 1812 or 2012?), highlighting all of Emma's flaws (and adding some new ones) without suggesting any of her virtues.  As the series goes on it's increasingly difficult to know what Mr. Knightley sees in her other than her beauty (RG definitely looks the part, I just wish she would act it as well).  Austen would cringe watching RG screech, bounce, and 'um, no' her way through the film.<br /><br />But the rest of the cast is so wonderfully engaging that it makes 'Emma' hard to resist (just Emma herself. Or 'Emmar' as Garai pronounces it).  Read the book again, then sit back and enjoy watching Highbury come to life on film, and ignore the Mean Girl in the title role.

It reminded me a bit of HeroQuest, a board game I loved as a kid. For example, if my oldest is using the fighter character with an AC of 17  and he's fighting someone with an attack of +8, he knows he wants to roll an 8 or lower for the monster's attack. B00756GKBA|5.0|I must preface this review by saying I've been a fan of Madonna's since the first time I heard Holiday in the fall of 1983, but that in no way means I love everything she's done in the last (almost) 30 years and, unlike many other fans of my generation, do not automatically give her music 5-star stellar reviews. Now that that's out of the way, I can't express how pleased I am with her return to form with MDNA. Despite how much I looked forward to the release of this record--four years is a long wait between new Madonna music, despite the bones she threw us with the two new songs off of Celebration (loved Celebration, couldn't stand Revolver)-- I was *very* skeptical when Give Me All Your Luvin' was released. Mediocre and an obvious a tie-in to the Super Bowl performance, I feared the sound of the song was a prelude to what was to come on the album. Thankfully my fears were totally unfounded. As soon as I heard Girl Gone Wild, I breathed a sigh of relief. Even though initially it sounded a little too much like everything else that's out now in the same genre, I soon warmed to it and heard the Madonna sound I know & love shine through.<br />Every Madonna album has at least a few songs that are filler and just not that great, but when she gets it right, she really gets it right, and this happens so often on MDNA that the missteps can be forgiven. I think what I love most about the album is that it's very traditional in that it has a nice balance of uptempo songs and beautiful ballads. Falling Free ranks up there amongst her best of the latter category-melodic, beautifully sung, and lyrically rich. Masterpiece is a nice ballad too, but Falling Free blows it out of the water. I F'ed Up and Best Friend are ballads with a beat, and even though it's impossible not to interpret the messages of both as quasi-tributes to the Guy Ritchie marriage, they're still good. The uptempo standouts: Girl Gone Wild, I'm Addicted, I'm A Sinner, Love Spent, and Beautiful Killer. I've read in SO many reviews that Madonna isn't ahead of the curve musically anymore, that she's just making music that's already popular, yadda yadda yadda...okay, and so what? Not everything she does can be revolutionary, and that's fine. Most of us just want to hear the Madonna sound, even it's if couched within \\"popular\\" music and beats, and MDNA has very much of the Madonna sound--especially after listening to the album a few times this fact becomes really apparent. She has a way of putting her own spin on whatever she does, and it's almost impossible define the Madonna sound, but you know it when you hear it.<br />The only songs that are just plain bad are I Don't Give A and B-Song (why she chose to include either I have no idea, but at this point in her career it's certainly her prerogative to do so, just as it is the listener's to hit the skip button when these clunkers start). It still doesn't take away from a 5-star review because, as I stated, it wouldn't be a Madonna album without at least a couple \\"Huh?!?\\" songs, and the great ones make these missteps pretty easy to ignore.<br />There's no one who can touch Madonna-and no, I'm not comparing her to Gaga, or Britney, or Ke$ha, or any of the many others who wouldn't have a career if it weren't for her influence (and props to the ones who freely admit this fact). Madonna never invented the wheel musically, but she's certainly carved out her own style and a place for herself in the music world: the 300+ million albums sold, 12 #1 (US) singles, world's top-selling female recording artist ever, and litany of other achievements more than attest to this fact. Very few artists in any medium are still relevant--not to mention undeniable popular icons--more than 30 years after coming onto the scene. Of course the haters will say she's not relevant, too old, this, that & the other: I've heard it all before (to inadvertently quote Sorry) and it's irrelevant. She's back and not going anywhere. No one's forcing people who don't want to listen to her music to do so. Luckily for those of us who do, we have a welcome return to form in MDNA and this makes me very happy.ant to listen to her music to do so. At the same time, Isaacson does a fairly good job of keeping himself from being sucked in to Jobs' \\"reality distortion field\\" - a technique that Jobs had of bending popular perception so that even people to whom he was quite unkind tended to view him as an \\"enlightened being\\" (a direct quote from his former girlfriend, whom he abandoned when she was pregnant with his child).<br /><br />Isaacson also captures, however, Jobs' genius for cutting to the heart of issues. A good biography can point out the human failings of a great figure in history while clarifying what made that person great, and I think this book does an excellent job on both counts. Reading \\"Steve Jobs\\" walks us through the development of the PC market, the MP3 market, the smartphone and tablet market - and illustrates the appeal of Apple products (even to someone like me, who is by-and-large no particularly enamored of Apple products).
1451683405|1.0|If you are like me, and must have an absolute answer to the mechanisms of life and all its mysteries, this little book fails to deliver (and I feel no one can find an absolute answer on consciousness, for the matter).<br /><br />For a neuroscientist, he only cites one measurable scientific study claiming to observe the brain making decisions before the person is aware, and lambastes various credible researchers with just this one study alone. It is Benjamin Libet's brain motor cortex study, in which participants were given a simple visual test of pinpointing which of two letters were visible on a screen. The researchers monitored their brain waves via EEG and fMRI, respectively. The results show the brain made decisions 7-10 seconds before the person was aware of it.<br /><br />Pretty straightforward, right? It shuts down any other argument on the origins of consciousness, yes? Not quite. First of all, it doesn't deny the existence of a separate agency, a soul, if you will; it's just another discovery of brain mechanisms at work. Second of all, a similar study was done, by Davide Rigoni. The subjects were given a \\"Libet task\\": pressing a button whenever and however many times they chose, while indicating on a screen the time they became aware of the intention to act. Only this time, the group was split into two, and read pre-narratives, one which said free will is an illusion, the other just mentioning consciousness, but not suggesting it is an illusion.<br /><br />The EEG findings indicate the brains of the control group, the one given the neutral narrative, had far more activity than the ones prepped with free will as an illusion. Their belief had a measurable impact on something Harris claims one cannot control. A separate agency within them decided free will is real, and their brains behaved accordingly. Or was the positive narrative just an environmental influence on soulless automatons? Depending on how you conduct the study, and how the data is perceived, onecan draw either conclusion. But I don't want to wait till the end of days to find the one answer; I'll just believe I have free will and enjoy myself!

B005FVFWOI|3.0|It's wonderful that the CD has been given a much better re-mix, and it's nice to have all the extras (not that most of them weren't in Tull fans' collections already in various formats), but let's be realistic about one huge point: this is probably the most overrated album in Jethro Tull's discography. The brilliant jazz-blues/improvisational hybrid of their first three albums and early singles is largely missing here (unsurprisingly: newcomer Jeffrey Hammond-Hammond on bass is no Glenn Cornick) in favor of a mix of some nice, but inconsequential acoustic numbers (\\"Wondering Aloud\\" is, well, wonderful, but \\"Cheap Day Return\\" and \\"Slipstream\\" barely qualify as songs, and \\"Mother Goose\\" is as silly as the title would suggest), and the bombastic arena-rock that would bring them fame and fortune, but at the cost of permanently abandoning the style they had been developing as a band: from here on in, it's the Ian Anderson show, with whatever supporting cast he has on hand to supplement himself and Martin Barre. The changes did come to fruition on the next effort, Thick as a Brick, which completed the renovation of the rhythm section with much more \\"prog\\" oriented drummer Barriemore Barlow, but this is largely a transitional album, and should be taken as such, not as the pinnacle of their achievements: everything that came before, and much of what came after, is simply better done.

B0040MF1XK|5.0|This as a superb set, perhaps the best of the available complete cycles of Beethoven sonatas.  Lortie maintains a remarkably high standard of performance through the whole cycle.  He plays with great power and conviction, and without any eccentricities.  His articulation is very clear, for instance in Beethoven's \\"pizzicato\\" bass passages where the left hand plays short, detached notes while the upper \\"voices\\" have a long, sustained melody (Most pianists fudge these to some extent, but with Lortie they are consistently clear).  And Lortie always seems to know where he is going, building momentum and intensity over long passages.  The lesser-known sonatas receive fine performances here (No.4 is particularly joyous), and the greatest ones do as well.  The first movement of the Moonlight (taken slowly) has great atmosphere and intensity, and the Waldstein is resplendent.  Indeed, Lortie's Op.101 may be the best I've ever heard.  Even the Hammerklavier comes off very convincingly. The layout of the set is perfect, with all the sonatas except No.4 in numerical order, and the opus-number sets like Op.10 and Op.31 together on a CD, and the sound quality is excellent, resonant in the usual Chandos style but with great detail and impact.<br />That being said, no one pianist can hope to reveal every facet of these amazing masterworks, and good as Lortie's set is, no collector will want to be without the many, many great recordings of individual sonatas (Arrau's Pathetique, Rubinstein's Moonlight, Richter's Tempest, Gilels' Waldstein, Horowitz's Appassionata, etc., etc.).  Highly recommended!
B006X08EZY|5.0|David Byrne and Caetano Veloso both work in the upper register. They share a borderless sense of music, one minute in America, the next in Brazil. Pairing them in concert at Carnegie Hall --- a no-brainer.<br /><br />Veloso's songs start in the ether; there's no thrust, no urgency. He's in the transportation business; his songs don't come to you, you come to them. And when you arrive, you're wrapped in his thoughts and moods.<br /><br />Byrne's trickier. When he was the kingpin of Talking Heads, he wrote his share of off-kilter hits; he can, at will, get you dancing. But he's shy, maybe aloof, and diffident --- no one's more self-consciously cool than a New York art rocker.<br /><br />My wife and I saw the 2004 Veloso-Byrne concert from about the tenth row. It was magic, spectacular right from the from start --- I think pretty much everyone there got that, and felt privileged, and went nuts with pleasure and gratitude after each song.  A while back, we ran into Byrne at a gallery and asked about a CD. \\"Soon,\\" he said. \\"Maybe.\\" Well, what's eight years --- half as long as it takes for single malt to be drinkable.<br /><br />Veloso begins the show. If you're new to him, you might not immediately grasp that Brazilians rank him in the Bob Dylan zone. Then the sweetness and sensitivity seduce you. Byrne's presence comes almost as a shock. But not really, because there's no band here, just cello and percussion --- the Talking Heads songs you know and love do rock, just more intimately. Byrne and Veloso do some duets. Byrne sings in Portuguese. The words really don't matter; this evening was really about an exploration of sound. Namely, about how many different ways you can achieve gorgeousness.<br /><br />We live in the land of reality TV competitions; we can't help ourselves from asking who won. These guys are way beyond that. Serve this music with wine at dinner. And don't fail to savor it again, late in the evening; in the dark, it is the wine.

You get evolutionary biology, the scientific method, chance and random events analysis, a little bit of molecular biology, some astronomy, earth evolution history (with earthquakes), and a mini \\"God Delusion\\". The book is written in an easy language and not condescending, although some reviewers complain about that. So far so good.<br /><br />Where the book really shines though, is in the illustrations. Davis McKean does a fantastic job not only in illustrating the text, but he actually enhances it. Evolution is illustrated with a bookshelf of ancestors' photographs and trees, the periodic table is brought to life with colors and also detailed electrons drawings, seasons and rainbows are explained with geometric diagrams. All the drawings are extremely well crafted and almost never redundant.<br /><br />So even if you don't care for Dawkins, or if you know everything that's in the book (I think most people will still find a couple of things they didn't know, or at least illuminating ways of explaining them), the illustrations are what really make the book a great and fun read. Skip the audiobook version.

1594203334|4.0|This is one of a genre of books that point out how people live better once they've moved away -- to a villa in Tuscany or to raising children in Paris. 0312641893|5.0|Cinder is a wonderfully fresh young adult, sci-fi, fairy tale retelling. Is that a sentence you ever thought you would read? New author Marissa Meyer retells the classic tale of Cinderella using cyborgs and androids - and I was captivated every moment.<br /><br />Cinder is set far, far into the future in New Beijing, a part of the Eastern Commonwealth. The Commonwealth is one of the six countries on Earth now, after World War IV basically led to continents coming together as united countries. Somewhere along the line, people from Earth colonized the moon and have since evolved into more than regular Earthlings. The Lunar people, as they are called, are cruel and possess the power to bend other people's thoughts. Cinder is a cyborg, someone who used to be a regular human but was in a near-death accident, after which she underwent a surgery that combined metal parts and computers with her human body. She lives with a horrible stepmother and one just as horrible stepsister. Her other stepsister, Peony, is sweet and kind to Cinder (I loved Peony). Cinder's only friend is her android, Iko, whose unusual personality made me smile. One day, while manning her mechanic booth in town, she meets the prince of the Commonwealth, Prince Kai, and agrees to fix his old android that stopped working recently. Prince Kai is dealing with his own issues. His father, the Emperor, has come down with the plague that has been attacking Earthlings. And the sinister Lunar Queen is making life even harder.<br /><br />What really drew me into this story was Cinder herself. She is treated awfully, just as the original Cinderella. She's not annoying and she doesn't make stupid choices like some other young adult heroines. Cinder is smart, strong, caring, and sometimes funny. Her stepmother and stepsister treat her like a possession, not even allowing her to have the proper mechanical body parts. I felt so terrible for her and my heart strings were pulled many times throughout the story. I also really loved all of the interactions between Cinder and Prince Kai. There was no \\"I just met you but I love you\\". Cinder repeatedly refuses his attention since she doesn't think he will still be interested when he finds out she's a cyborg. I can tell that their budding romance is going to continue to build over the course of the four book series and that makes me very happy. I admit that I wasn't really shocked by any revelations during the story, but I found myself not really minding. I was enjoying the story so much that some of the predictable elements just made the story more fun! The way Meyer's story unfolds is surprising and exciting. During the last few chapters of the story I was practically holding my breath!<br /><br />With believable characters, a plot that somehow feels refreshing despite the fact that it's a retelling, and an ending that makes me WISH the next book was coming out anytime soon, I highly recommend Cinder!

0307592731|5.0|WILD is a fearlessly told, wildly fantastic, and entertaining story. The author, Cheryl Strayed (not her birth name, but one she chose after a her divorce) exceeded any and all expectations I had when Wild arrived in my mailbox. This is a memoir, but reads like a novel. All human behavior, interaction, and communication, I think, is best understood via the narrative, in other words--a story. And so I am going to talk about this story as if it were fiction - in the language of the six fundamental elements of a story.<br /><br />Title. Perfect. To look at the spine is to see 'Cheryl Strayed WILD'. Which would induce me to pluck it off any shelf. [However, I think the cover would have been better with a snapshot of Cheryl, or a trail maker of the PCT; but I understand the choice of the boot.] Cheryl Strayed is a wild girl, as she says: \\"I was an experimentalist ...  An artist. The kind of woman who said yes instead of no.\\" (p. 54)  That is a trail marker letting the reader know just what kind of journey you are about to get into.<br /><br />Plot. One of the best - Redemption of the human soul through the force of will, strength, and toughness. Briefly, Cheryl was born in 1968, white, female, attractive, intelligent, and in addition had the benefit of a college education. You could call her advantaged. But, big but, she was born into rural poverty and domestic violence and then her champion, advocate, and sole support - her mother - died suddenly of cancer when Cheryl was twenty-two. Cheryl, lost, descended into debauchery, seemingly bent on self- destruction via sex and drugs.<br /><br />Characterization. Not only did I quickly begin to root for Cheryl, I fell in love with her. She was/is the personification of my \\"perfect\\" woman. [I am aware, wistfully, that the person I am in love with is a fiction--a 26 year-old woman with the wisdom and wit of the same person 15 years later.] Not only was she white, young, attractive, intelligent, and funny; she was by her declaration: \\"--strong and responsible, clear-eyed and driven, ethical and good.\\" (p. 57) [And] \\"I was a big fat idiot and I didn't know what the hell I was doing ...\\" (p.58)  [Told you she was funny.] And it just keeps getting better, funnier; and yet in the next sentence she is as likely to bring tears to my eyes as to make me laugh out loud.<br /><br />Point of view. Her voice is perfect. I can hear her, see her! In all her joy and sadness and frustration, in other words, in all her humanity ... or femaleness. Cheryl blends the past with the present in the telling of her story masterfully. There is no confusion for the reader. Her wisdom and humor and reflection merge in ways that are really rare in writing.<br /><br />Setting. B000063UR1|4.0|Boy, have there been some scathing reviews in this forum of this film, but a modest budget was in play when it was shot, so back off a little. Besides 2001: A Space Odyssey, Silent Running was one of those few childhood cinematic events that drove me to the theater on at least three more occasions to bask in its excellence after it was first released. Everything about it was seemingly right on the money, science fiction-wise, and one viewing wasn't enough. And when it finally hit prime time a few short years later, nothing was lost in the hand-off to the little screen except the big screen experience. Irksome though was the use of the exterior shots of the film's spaceship in an episode of the first Battlestar Gallactica series.<br /><br />Well, I finally went ahead and bought the 2002 DVD release of 'Running a few years ago and had trouble with some of the now immediate chintzy elements of the film that are hard to forgive, like: Bruce Dern's less-than-adequate-looking bodysuit-space suit when he's outside the ship talking to a robot (called \\"drones\\" in the film); the camera pulling away from the spaceship Valley Forge as Dern is seen preparing a meal through a large window that entirely disappears when the shot transitions to a larger view of the ship; the goofiness of the 20th century electronics surrounding Dern in the ship's interiors; and some of Dern's wild-eyed histrionics, especially as he tries to solicit sympathy from his apathetic shipmates for a child in a photo who will never experience nature due to their employer's resolve to destroy their ship's cargo (the tears Dern's crumpled face tries to squeeze out never come in perhaps one of the hammiest scenes ever delivered by a thespian; Hormel and Farmer John would be proud).<br /><br />But that's about all the negative critique I can muster. The rest of the film was bitchin'. The use of (spoiler alert) amputees for the three drones was about as clever as it gets. There was a naturalness in the interplaybetween Dern and the three other actors on board the Valley Forge: Cliff Potts, Jesse Vint, and Ron Rifkin. The latter group's eagerness to \\"blow the pods\\" and get back down to Earth, home, and regular lives aids the pathos of Dern's actions and the pacing of the film's plot. Using a real-life, decommissioned aircraft carrier (the actual Valley Forge) for the ship's interiors gave the film a nifty scope in terms of the ship's size. The perilous crossing through Saturn's rings was probably about the best visual special effects that could be mustered at the time. When Dern and crew arms the nuclear detonators, the tension is high, and the resultant explosions are a fair enough fireworks show. And let's not forget the over-the-radio voices of Roy Engel and Joseph Campanella (\\"Big Billy wants to go!\\") as the officious types prodding Dern on to stoic resolve. They're perfect but sadly uncredited for their great work. And using hippy era-hold over Joan Baez to warble two fine songs during key moments in the film was genuine inspiration-she never sounded better.<br /><br />The big plus is Dern. Before 'Running, he'd toiled for years as an actor in small parts in big productions, both on film and in television. This was undoubtedly his breakout role, and (besides what's already been noted here) he acts his heart out. He soon became a big enough name in the kinds of parts that finally complimented the longevity of his career. It's evident that 'Running would certainly have suffered with any most other fledgling actors besides Dern in the lead. He brought loads of acting experience to the affair that made it that more terrific.<br /><br />Lastly, any film helmed by Douglas Trumbull, a guy who rubbed shoulders with Kubrick during the filming of the greatest science fiction motion picture ever made, can't be all bad. If he had just had a little more money... .

B004J4WLB4|5.0|This story shows the complexity and brokenness of human nature through the eyes of a young girl. Victoria was abandoned at birth by her mother. She never knew her mother or her father. Jerked from foster care to foster care, she understandably lacks stability, love and self-esteem.<br /><br />Year after year her insecurity and hesitancy to attach to any human being grows. Any human touch causes Victoria nausea and potential vomiting. Until she meets Elizabeth at age 10. Elizabeth is a vineyard owner and becomes her temporary guardian. This home is finally where Victoria begins to feel what real love might feel like.<br /><br />I realize the book is not categorized as inspirational/religious, but I saw unconditional love played out in the part of Elisabeth. Elisabeth told Victoria repeatedly that there was nothing she could do to cause her to give her up or to not love her. Victoria still tries the limits of this love on more than one occasion. Yet Elisabeth never withdraws her love or gives up on winning her back again. Just like God never gives up on us no matter how many times we mess up. He's always willing to take us back under his wing of protection.<br /><br />Then came the fire, hospitalization for Victoria, and Elisabeth disappeared from Victoria's life. Unfortunately, she's left with no choice, so it's back into the flawed foster child system where she stays until she becomes emancipated from a group home.<br /><br />A form of redemption came into Victoria's life when she met Renata, owner of Bloom flower shop. Renata hires Victoria to work for her at the flower shop. Renata seems to understand Victoria's complexity and need for her own space. Victoria begins to bloom and blossom as she creates arrangements according to the client's needs -- almost to a magical point. Client's personal relationships improved according to the flowers Victoria's arranged and delivered. Hence, the flower shop prospered and Victoria matures.<br /><br />The author goes into great detail about plants and flowers describing their unique meanings. I looked forward to learning, along with Victoria, the history and thinking behind each flower and grew to love flowers even more as I read the story. \\"The Victorian language of flowers was used to convey romantic expressions: honeysuckle for devotion, aster for patience, and red roses for love. But for Victoria Jones, it's been more useful in communicating grief, mistrust, and solitude.\\"<br /><br />The main character, Victoria, deals with deep-rooted woundedness and unresolved self-esteem issues her entire life. She resists anyone who tries to get close to her emotionally or physically by acting out in uncivil ways. As a reader, I personally felt the pain of the underdog, the unwanted, unloved and pushed aside in society. I wanted to reach out and adopt Victoria myself!<br /><br />The Language of Flowers deals with complex human nature and social issues. I found the book to be very intriguing and swept me right along from start to finish.

143512975X|4.0|My feelings about this book are nicely summed up by Dorian Gray's description of a hedonistic book he has received from Lord Henry Wotton: \\"I didn't say I liked it...I said it fascinated me.  There is a great difference.\\"<br /><br />The basic plot of the story is well known: thanks to mysterious/magical intervention the physical consequences of Dorian's action affect his portrait rather than his physical body.  Urged on by Lord Henry Wotton (whose shocking/amusing/wicked epigrams are classic Oscar Wilde), Dorian descends into a life of ever-increasing debauchery, manipulation, hedonism, and the love of beauty above all else.<br /><br />Wilde brings up a number of themes in sometimes dizzying succession: the ability to \\"dominate\\" others, beauty as the supreme virtue, self-actualization as the guide to morality, yielding to temptation as a means of getting beyond temptation, physical consequences vs. consequences in general, the fascination of wickedness, etc.  It is often unclear where Wilde stands on these matters since their proponent is Wotton who is \\"wicked\\" and the cause of much suffering, but speaks in the manner of Oscar Wilde.  The moral ambiguity of the story lets the reader draw their own conclusions...fascinating.

031618201X|5.0|`Soulless' is the Yen Press manga adaptation of the first book in Gail Carriger's `Parasol Protectorate / Alexia Tarabotti' steampunk paranormal series. Art and adaptation is by Rem, lettering by JuYoun Lee.<br /><br />I love the recent trend of graphic novel adaptations. I really enjoyed Dabel Brothers `Mercy Thompson' adaptation, and I especially loved Del Rey's adaptation of Diana Gabaldon's `The Exile'. Now, I know that lots book fans haven't really embraced the graphic adaptations, for a number of reasons - some of them are valid (such as not liking character drawings to intrude and determine reader's imaginations) to the ridiculous (like lashing out at authors because they assume the graphic novel is the next book in the series, not an adaptation of the first book?!). Whatever your stance on the graphic novel debate, I truly believe that everyone should at least give these adaptations a chance, because they might surprise you. And if you're dipping your toes in the adaptation waters for the first time, I can highly recommend this manga representation of Carriger's steampunk series.<br /><br />I was curious at reading a manga `comic', when I have only ever read (and enjoyed) graphic novels. But I actually think the more comic styling of manga suits Carriger's over-the-top series. For those who are unaware - graphic novels are considered more `books' than comics, often more text-heavy and more realistic drawing representations. The Japanese word `manga' literally means comic/graphic/cartoon, so is basically the same as a graphic novel. . .  except the manga format looks less real, more anime (I guess you could say it's the difference between a drawn cartoon show, and a CGI movie?).<br /><br />Carriger's steampunk series is perfectly suited to the manga style. For one thing, the `Parasol Protectorate' book series is so rich in outlandish description (from mechanical ladybugs to luxury dirigibles) that seeing these gorgeous steampunk contraptions drawn to life will be absolutely splendid! The little drawings of steampunk machinery throughout `Soulless' are lovely and fantastical, and exactly how I pictured London when reading Carriger's books! Admittedly, `Soulless' features less of those lovely steampunk inventions than latter books in the series - indeed, this first book is more about setting up the players and meeting the characters. And I must say, artist Rem has drawn each and every one of them to a T!<br /><br />Alexia is beautifully represented, in all her big-busted, parasol-wielding glory. Actually, quite a lot is made of Alexia's *ahem* assets. . .  but it's often for a bit of light relief, like when Conall is bending over to examine a dead vampire, and just so happens to cop a lovely eyeful down Alexia's corset. I really loved Aexia's drawn fashion too - her dresses are Victorian delights, and her long locks are always a little bit unkempt and wispy (staying very true to Carriger's descriptions of her in the books). Speaking of style. . . . I also love, love, loved the mangafied Ivy Tunstell! Rem has indeed captured her outlandish dress sense; her hats are divinely flouncy and there are ruffles aplenty!<br /><br />And then there's Conall (Lord Maccon). At first I wasn't overly thrilled with his appearance - I guess I wanted him a little more rugged and Alpha manly? And I thought his semi-wolf `angry face' looked a little ridiculous (his actual werewolf does look more like a frightening wolf, it's just when he's snarling at Alexia that the ears and tail pop out for some reason). My scepticism turned to appreciation towards the end. . .  as people who read `Soulless' will remember, at the end of the book werewolf Conall is captured, and thrown into a cell with Alexia. . .  whose touch turns him human (and very, very naked). And *that* is the Conall I was imagining! He's absolutely scrumptious in his naked manga glory - all broad chest, rippling abs, not to mention firm in all the right places (insert lascivious wink).<br /><br />My other favourite drawn character was Lord Akeldama - Alexia's vampire friend and delectable cream puff. Rem has drawn a fabulously flamboyant Akeldama, and I especially liked that he's so damn attractive!<br /><br />`Soulless' is one of the best adaptations I have ever read! The manga style perfectly suits Gail Carriger's over-the-top fabulous steampunk paranormal series; from the Victorian clothes to the roped dirigibles in the park. I especially loved that the manga adaptation covers every aspect of the first book! Even if you're not so sure about illustrated adaptations, give `Soulless' a read - I think lots of sceptics will be very surprised at how wonderful this adaptation is.).<br /><br />My other favourite drawn character was Lord Akeldama - Alexia's vampire friend and delectable cream puff. Rem has drawn a fabulously flamboyant Akeldama, and I especially liked that he's so damn attractive!<br /><br />`Soulless' is one of the best adaptations I have ever read! The manga style perfectly suits Gail Carriger's over-the-top fabulous steampunk paranormal series; from the Victorian clothes to the roped dirigibles in the park. I especially loved that the manga adaptation covers every aspect of the first book! Even if you're not so sure about illustrated adaptations, give `Soulless' a read - I think lots of sceptics will be very surprised at how wonderful this adaptation is.

B0000CD5I6|4.0|This is a pretty enjoyable album, if not on a par with classic Tull (or even contemporary Tull/Ian Anderson) in my opinion.  The songs fall into 3 categories:<br /> -7 rerecordings of classic Tull songs with a Christmassy theme/feel.  These were great songs the first time around, and are still pretty good, but if you're a Tull fan, you probably already have a (superior) version of them.  I find the lighter, more sophisticated, touch of the originals actually was much more Christmassy on songs from the 70s than the current production/sound,  while the later songs (e.g. \\"Another Christmas Song\\") don't sound too different.  Although Ian's vocals are certainly not on a par with his singing pre-1984, he does well enough here. The one standout is \\"Jack Frost & the Hooded Crow\\", which sounds fantastic here in a more robust recording than the original (which was a non-album, early 80s track that can be found on the 20th Ann. set or Broadsword remaster).  Bouree here gets a makeover to make it a bit different, with some time-signature and syncopation changes, which makes it more worth a new recording.<br />  - 5 instrumental traditional Christmas melodies (including a classical piece by Faure).  While I would love to hear Tull do these in a Renaissancy, Songs From The Wood, kind of way, the band instead decides to do a kind of cheesy jazz interpretation of them, which doesn't really work nearly as well for me.  The exception is the Faure \\"Pavanne\\", which is quite lovely (and not jazzed out).<br />  3.  The remaining 4 tracks are new Tull tunes, the only ones we've gotten for 13 years and counting!  \\"Birthday Card At Christmas\\" is a decent rock tune that would've fit on a recent Tull album.  \\"Last Man At The Party\\" is a quite good, fun, acousticy song about relatives overindulging.  \\"First Snow on Brooklyn\\" is an OK, mellow tune reminiscent of \\"White Innocence\\" or some of the last 1/3 of \\"Roots To Branches\\".  \\"A Winter Snowscape\\" is an excellent, lovely instrumental by MartinBarre, with Ian's flute complimenting what I believe was originally a Barre solo album piece.<br /><br />Overall, an enjoyable Tull album that's Christmas-themed but not too Christmassy to listen to out-of-season.  Not brilliant, but pretty good and with some great stuff.  (It may be noted that this is not a consistent effort by the current lineup of Tull - keyboardist Andy Giddings seems to be handling most of the bass guitar on the vocal songs, while the instrumentals have James Duncan filling in on drums.  This does not seem to harm the continuity of the album, however).

B00001OH7U|5.0|The circumstances in David Bowie's life while making STATION TO STATION are often commented on: his use of drugs had got out of control, and he has later claimed not to remember a thing about its production. Taking his personal problems at the time into consideration, it is quite striking (and probably rather a compliment to his talent than to the drug-glamorizing sometimes found in rock'n'roll) that this turned out to be possibly his most interesting and consistent album. Some reviewers have remarked that such a good album would have benefited from displaying more tracks than a mere six, but I think some of the beauty of STATION TO STATION is found precisely in its relative brevity. Running at less than 40 minutes in total, the album provides a feeling of punctuality which makes the listener more attentive to its complex nuances and references; we all know that pure, simple rock'n'roll may go on forever, but a work like STATION TO STATION demands a certain degree of focus in order to be fully appreciated, which would likely become exhausting had two or three more tracks of equal quality been present. As it stands, it is one of the most homogeneous albums I've yet heard; and one of the most intense, unpredictable, and sometimes slightly bizarre.<br /><br />The title track, which unfolds the album, is Bowie's longest song in his career, as has been noted many times, and also one of his most complex. It builds itself up gradually, the mood being first set by slow, claustrophobic piano, and Bowie declaring \\"The Return of The Thin White Duke,\\" before eventually taking off in an uptempo rythm with Bowie exclaiming one of his most oft-quoted lines: \\"It's not the side effects of the cocaine/I'm thinking that it must be love...\\" The references are too numerous to be grasped initially by any normal listener; gnosticism and Aleister Crowley are said to have been some of Bowie's influences here. Both lyrically and musically, it's undoubtedly one of his most interesting pieces. However, running at over ten minutes and with Bowie's voice absent for the first three, \\"Station to Station\\" was an impossible hit single choice, which may have been one reason why it's followed up by the most accessible track on the album, \\"Golden Years.\\" This song is sometimes critized for slightly interrupting the flow of the album, but I think it works as a fitting intermission between the complex introduction and the next, highly emotional song \\"Word on a Wing.\\" Bowie exclaiming the refrain \\"My prayer flies like a word on a wing\\" is one of his most gripping moments, and the outro of the song, with the tuned-down chorus, once tempted a few tears out of me.<br /><br />\\"TVC15\\" may be the song here I listen to the most; Bowie is at his vocally most vigorous here, which is highlighted by the (again) tuned-down saxophone towards the end of the refrains. \\"Stay\\" appears to be a follow-up to \\"Word on a Wing,\\" at least in terms of mood, though it sounds slightly less desperate. Finally, \\"Wild is the Wind\\" is admittedly the song I listen to the least here, possibly because Bowie does not provide the lyrics (with such a good lyricist, you somehow want him to have thought up every sentence...am I spoiled?), but nonetheless it serves as a very fitting conclusion to the album, Bowie making the song into his own through his incredible vocal range. When released, STATION TO STATION was generally received with applaud, and its reputation holds up to this day. There's less pure rock'n'roll to be found than in the Ziggy Stardust-era, but rather the visions of a maturing artist.However, running at over ten minutes and with Bowie's voice absent for the first three, \\"Station to Station\\" was an impossible hit single choice, which may have been one reason why it's followed up by the most accessible track on the album, \\"Golden Years.\\" This song is sometimes critized for slightly interrupting the flow of the album, but I think it works as a fitting intermission between the complex introduction and the next, highly emotional song \\"Word on a Wing.\\" Bowie exclaiming the refrain \\"My prayer flies like a word on a wing\\" is one of his most gripping moments, and the outro of the song, with the tuned-down chorus, once tempted a few tears out of me.<br /><br />\\"TVC15\\" may be the song here I listen to the most; Bowie is at his vocally most vigorous here, which is highlighted by the (again) tuned-down saxophone towards the end of the refrains. \\"Stay\\" appears to be a follow-up to \\"Word on a Wing,\\" at least in terms of mood, though it sounds slightly less desperate. Finally, \\"Wild is the Wind\\" is admittedly the song I listen to the least here, possibly because Bowie does not provide the lyrics (with such a good lyricist, you somehow want him to have thought up every sentence...am I spoiled?), but nonetheless it serves as a very fitting conclusion to the album, Bowie making the song into his own through his incredible vocal range. When released, STATION TO STATION was generally received with applaud, and its reputation holds up to this day. There's less pure rock'n'roll to be found than in the Ziggy Stardust-era, but rather the visions of a maturing artist.

B007IXWL2C|3.0|No surprises here: a rich alpha male with a harrowing childhood and inner demons plucks (I choose that word deliberately) a young woman from obscurity, or in this case, college. Christian Grey decides in a few weeks that Ana Steele will be his salvation to his inner demons and is the love of his life.  Ana decides in as short a time that he is the love of her life.  If she didn't, Grey offers her the money, the sex drive, and the emotional sustenance to ensure she will.  Ana and Grey are also, Ms James assures us repeatedly, very good looking.  Ana doesn't stand a chance, and neither do we.<br /><br />Most people may focus, wrongly in my view, that sex is the only element of these books.  Don't get me wrong: there's a lot of it, it's well-written (a rarity), and it will probably introduce a lot of readers to BDSM-oriented romance.  For what it's worth, I'm more offended by Grey's taste in classical music than I am by his handcuffs.  Ignore the pearl clutchers and read the trilogy if you're interested.  It's handled responsibly.<br /><br />But it's Grey and Ana's highly emotional connection that drives their relationship, and the book.  It has to.  There's little plot or character development.  To compensate for this lack, Grey and Ana emote in a way that is simply breathtaking.  Ana cries, shakes and goes off her feed at the slightest whiff of trouble.  Grey storms about in anger, professes love and talks about sex in a straightforward manner that modern men rarely do - and I suspect, readers wish they did.  Some romance sub-genres get away by turning up the emotions to level 11 by making the stakes high - the fate of a clan might hang in the balance in a historical romance, the death of a \\"one true mate\\" or the end of the world in a paranormal.  But by placing this romance in modern time and not creating a substitute reason for the drama, the emotional toils, the alpha male posturing and the female responses (Ana rarely drives the action, or the cars) all become melodrama.<br /><br />Simply put - the stakes are never high enough to justify the high emotions, despite Ms James frantic attempts to tell us differently.  The reader is well aware after just a few pages of the first book that Ms James would never put her heroine in anything resembling true harm; she seems to have a hard enough time putting Ana in a bad dress.  Grey is never in any danger of losing his business empire, his loving adopted family or Ana.<br /><br />The conceit of a great romance is for a reader to know the ending will be happy but for the writer to be so wily as to make us forget.  Not so here, where my major concern was whether Ana will twist her heel in her new Christian Laboutins or have sex in boring place, like a bed.<br /><br />By making the stakes low and love a foregone conclusion, the resolution - such as it is - by book 3 feels as cheaply held as the string of Audi and Saabs Grey buys Ana.  (Yes, name brands have a starring role here, along with the handcuffs.)  Ana's personal evolution seems as empty.  Forget a bad dress, Ms James doesn't even let her experience the ordinary awkwardness of the post-college phase or enjoyment of simple pleasures.  Ana goes straight from a delayed adolescence - at 22 she is a virgin and socially awkward and un self-aware - to professional success, a warm social circle, and a sartorially-adept adulthood in a few short months.<br /><br />The sex and wish fulfillment make it fun; the dearth of story makes it empty.  There are better romances out there, but if you want pure frosting and fluff, this is about as sugary as it gets.

B005FITIF0|4.0|Machine Gun Preacher is one of those movies that sneaks up on you, grabs your heart, and doesn't let go. Going into this movie, I'm a bit embarrassed to say I not only hadn't heard of Sam Childers and his work over in Sudan and Uganda, but had no idea who Joseph Kony was, the leader of the LRA (Lord's Resistance Army). But I guess that is the point of movies like this-- to educate and inform people about very serious current issues going on in the world. As a blogger, a teacher, and the type of person that is not afraid to speak my mind, I knew before the end credits rolled that I would be reviewing Machine Gun Preacher and dedicating the post to the children of Uganda and Sudan and to spread awareness so that Kony's reign of terror will end.<br /><br />Gerard Butler does a great job portraying Sam Childers, former drug dealer, heroin addict, motorcycle misfit whose first moments on the screen is him being released after doing a stint in prison. I like the fact that the movie doesn't try to sugarcoat how Sam used to be--you can add verbally abusive to his wife and not much of a father figure to his daughter to the list. But after a life changing incident, he allows his wife to clean him up and take him to the church that helped her find God (she was also a former addict & stripper).<br /><br />Now many may think this movie is \\"preachy\\" since it shows Sam being baptized and he later builds his own church--but I'm setting the record straight, this movie's agenda is not to \\"convert\\" or \\"recruit\\" people to Christianity. This is not a film in the fashion of Fireproof and Courageous. The solid R rating should attest to that. But Sam IS a born again Christian and the movie IS about his life, so of course it's going to play an important part of the film. I just wanted to clear the air for those people that may have been thinking about skipping the film because they thought Machine Gun Preacher was going to be \\"preachy\\".<br /><br />I don't want to give too much away as far as describing specific scenes since I think this movie is much more effective by watching it with your own eyes, but let me say there are definitely some shocking moments when Sam is over in Africa. Children being maimed, tortured, burned, children forced to kill other children, etc. The one scene I will divulge that pissed me off-- as it did Sam-- was actually the scene where he asks a rich businessman who praised his work over in Africa, for $5,000 which would help keep the orphanage running for another 6 months. The man invites Sam and his family over to a huge, lavish party where he hands him an envelope. Sam excuses himself to the bathroom, opens it, to find a check for....$150 dollars! WTF?? Sometimes I forget how selfish and self centered people can be!<br /><br />What kept me from giving this movie a complete 5 star rating was more a directorial/writing/casting issue. The first thing that bothered me was the time transitions or lack there of. In the beginning of the movie, there was a little blurb that popped up to let everyone know what year it was. The rest of the film is not so kind. I'm not saying every scene needed a little blurb to let me know that time had passed, but there were definitely a few awkward time transitions that confused me. Most movies that are based on true stories will say something like, \\"2 years later\\" or something to help the viewer acclimate to the time change. In this movie, one minute Paige, Sam's daughter, is 5 years old, then she is suddenly in her young teens. This wouldn't be a big deal but I had to rewind it because while everyone else looked the same, there was an older actress playing Paige. I think a little blurb stating that time had passed would have been helpful.<br /><br />Another awkward scene is when Donnie, Sam's best friend, tries to take over his nightly duties as a father. It's sweet that while Sam was over in Africa, Donnie would keep an eye on Lynn and Paige. But there is just this part where Donnie is tellingPaige goodnight, and she asks, \\"Aren't you gonna read me a book?\\" Followed by her saying her dad normally kisses her on the forehead and he can do that if he wanted to. Now this isn't the 5 year old Paige asking this. It is the 13-14 year old Paige asking. I work with middle/high school kids and...I don't know, I just felt that that particular scene was creepy and awkward. Not many kids that age that I know would ask their dad's friend for a kiss on the forehead. That's just my opinion!<br /><br />My last issue is a small one, more like a goof. There is a scene where Sam is at the airport to leave for Africa and Lynn, Paige, and Donnie walk him right up to the departing gate. I think everyone knows that doesn't happen anymore, especially after 9/11.<br /><br />Like I said, these are just minor issues I had. 1401937519|5.0|A book review should concentrate on the book, not the reviewer, but in this case I think it would be appropriate to say a few things about myself at the outset - things that make me, if not unique, then at least rare among the other reviewers of this book. I have also written a number of books on NDEs and scores of articles on the subject.  In addition, I have had the pleasure, and indeed the privilege, of spending several hours with the author, Anita Moorjani, of the book under review here.  So I think I know something about the subject of NDEs.<br /><br />That said, I am prepared to say that Dying to be Me is perhaps the most compelling, insightful and deeply moving rendering of an NDE I have encountered over the past 35 years.  It is a book that not only tells an incredibly gripping story of an NDE and the healing from a disease that seemed destined to be fatal, but one that also offers a great deal of spiritual and practical wisdom that the author derived from her experience during her otherworldly sojourn.<br /><br />I haven't read any of the sixty of so reviews of this book (though I noticed that they are overwhelmingly of the 5-star variety), but I'm sure many of them must already inform the reader of the book's contents.  I have no need to be redundant on that score here, but for the record, Moorjani, a woman of Indian heritage, grew up in the multi-cultural setting of Hong Kong, eventually contracted cancer, suffered with it for four years and by February, 2006, was taken to a hospital and given no chance to survive the night.  However, when she was on the brink of death, she had an extraordinarily powerful NDE, which she recounts with impressive articulateness, and knewin the moment of her experience that she would be healed.  Indeed, that she would be completely cured.  As in fact she was, to the stupefaction of her oncologists.  (All is this documented in the book.)  Her life has not been the same since.<br /><br />Eventually, her story came to the attention of the well-known author, Wayne Dyer, who was so riveted by Anita's account that he spared no effort to track her down and once he did, he told her that she must write a book about her story and he would see to it that it would get published.  He did and it did and within a month of its publication, it had deservedly become a best seller.<br /><br />What more do I need to say?  Anita Moorjani is the real deal.  Her book is destined to become not only the best seller it already is but a classic among books on NDEs.  It will inspire you, thrill you, bring you comfort and bring you joy.  You will be deeply touched in so many ways by this book.  What are you waiting for?

B001GKYBXA|5.0|Paul McCartney lately has a daring musical sense of adventure and this album he recorded with British producer Youth has a brilliance and a lot of surprises from the 66 year old genius.  They called themselves THE FIREMAN and this is their third and best album to date.  From screaming vocals to classic pop to ambient sounds, it is an exhilarating ride that is puzzling in places but is full of McCartney's melodic hooks.  On a lot of tracks, McCartney's voice is recessed and has some echo lending an ethereal feel to the music. Also his voice is almost unrecognizeable on some songs: TRAVELLING LIGHT, NOTHING TOO MUCH...,  IS THIS LOVE?, LIGHT FROM YOUR LIGHTHOUSE don't sound like him at all.<br /><br />On the first track: NOTHING TOO MUCH JUST OUT OF SIGHT, Macca delivers a savage vocal, very bluesy and raw; almost as raucous as HELTER SKELTER. The next track: TWO MAGPIES is my least favourite and arguably the weakest on the album. It sounds lazy, tuneless and somewhat uninspired. Thankfully it is only just over two minutes long.<br /><br />But then we have six songs that I can listen to over and over again: from the anthemmic, joyous SING THE CHANGES to the moody, Leonard Cohen-esque TRAVELLING LIGHT which sounds like it is sung by an old, world-wise, weary man and that is not a criticism by any means. Then comes the rocking HIGHWAY with its infectious chorus and wonderful multi-tracked coda. Next is the simple but very likeable LIGHT FROM YOUR LIGHTHOUSE, which for all its simplicity, has an almost demonic harmony vocal in the verse. Another immediately catchy song is SUN IS SHINING which is all sunshine and joy with a wonderful opening depicting the sunrise and a very catchy na-na-na refrain. Then Macca and Youth do a Phil Spector wall of sound production on DANCE TILL WE'RE HIGH, which has a very seasonal, Christmas feel to it. This could have been a great Christmas song if the lyrics were different.<br /><br />The last five tracks are the ambient, new-age phase ofthe album. Very experimental with Paul exploring new effects and musical collages. Maybe some of it is too repetitive (as in LOVERS IN A DREAM) but it is definitely hypnotic and when I am in the right mood, it is a half-hour of total relaxation and mind travel. Not all of it is soft and ethereal as UNIVERSAL HERE, EVERLASTING NOW has a hi-energy sound.<br /><br />A couple of slight complaints:<br /><br />1. The last track DON'T STOP RUNNING, which is one of the best on the album has a playing time of 10:30 minutes. But the track is basically over after six minutes. Then there is a very long two minutes of silence before some synthesizer effects play out the last two minutes. If only an extra track could have been included to take up the last four minutes of the album...<br /><br />2. There is a large booklet that comes with the cd. i was hoping to find out more about the collaboration between Youth and McCartney as well as notes on the making of the album.  But instead the pages are full of McCartney working on his ho-hum paintings and the final works.<br /><br />But I loved the adventure that this album provides and for a guy who was 66 years old when he recorded the album, I still have the strongest admiration and appreciation for Sir Paul.

B002MQYOFW|4.0|I saw the movie the other day and was intrigued enough by the concept to want to read the book. It is a great premise for a dystopian novel. The \\"reaping\\" reminded me of the haunting classic short story by Shirley Jackson \\"The Lottery\\" and I was impressed to see how Collins expanded this concept and put such a fascinating twist to it. When reading a dystopian novel, one does have to extend their beliefs a bit -- satires like this are meant to magnify the darkest aspects of human nature and so I had no difficulty in accepting the premise of the storyline.<br />Children from the most repressed societies selected at random to fight to the death for the entertainment of the upper-classes. Here in America, where we send our poor and repressed to war to fight for us and where our entertainment largely comes from reality TV, it does not seem so far-fetched! But the plot isn't even that simple, the politics of the battle go beyond strategy and survival in the ring--participants (or Tributes) are further humiliated by having to secure sponsorships, which greatly increase their chances of survival, by trying to win the favor of the very people that put them there to suffer.<br /><br />While reading the book itself, I enjoyed it and was surprised to see that the movie remained largely true to the story. However, I was disappointed with the character development. I did not feel connected to any of them really--and often felt confused by them and their motives. There was so much I would have liked to have known about Peeta, about Haymitch, the other tributes, even Katniss the main character herself seemed to fall short in character development--and I got the feeling that the author meant to write this book as a vehicle to deliver a message rather than write a story.<br /><br />Even with the shortcomings, I am hooked and cannot wait to read the following books. 0307593312|4.0|Japan's Haruki Murakami writes a surreal novel for the intellectual reader with a touch of fantasy. This is not a completely lucid story, nor byzantine, so I would say it's semiabstract. Haruki may be the leader of a new genre. It's almost like he didn't write the novel for the hoi polloi, but for the artsy reader. Not that I'm a artsy reader! The story is basically strong, but leaves a lot of unanswered questions in the book's last 25 pages, after already reading 900 previously. I was a little disappointed with that scenario, since I became very interested in many of the characters and wanted closure one way or the other. Maybe I'm wrong and there will be a second book to answer my linguistic inquiries. But overall, I found the novel uniquely absorbing with strong character development.<br /><br />The story is set in Japan, mostly in the Tokyo area during the year 1984. Our heroine, Aomame ( Ah-oh-mah-meh ), who has the strange vocation of a sport's trainer and as a part time assassin is dropped off by a taxi on a traffic jammed expressway. She climbs down the elevated expressway via a strange stairway to a lower level. Once on the ground, her world is about to change. On her way to a assassination, she notices slight differences in policemen's uniforms and pistols. Later Aomame notices that she missed news events and the big one: Our moon suddenly has a companion! It's a smaller green moon! She realizes that she has entered a somewhat altered world. Our heroine calls it 1Q84 ( the Q for question ).<br /><br />Our hero in the novel is Tengo Kawana, a teacher and a nascent author, who as a ten year old went to school with Aomame. He rewrites a novel for a mysterious seventeen year old named Fuka-Eri. The book Air Chrysalis becomes a bestseller, but causes major problems with the religious group Sakigake. The group hires a Mr. Ushikawa ( a vile, but enjoyable character ) to dissuade Tengo from further involvement in the book. He refuses and this sets off a strife between Sakigake, Tengo and Fuka-Eri. Aomame gets enveloped in this trouble with Sakigake via a assignment from a arcane dowager and her bodyguard Mr. Tamaru. How all this comes together is a monumental accomplishment by the author. Other characters worth mentioning are Leader, Buzzcut, Ponytail and Professor Ebisuno. Character creation seems to be a strong suit of Haruki Murakami. I can't remember when I liked so many characters in a novel, I would probably have to go back to Charles Dickens's 'David Copperfield' !<br /><br />So as you can tell, I liked the novel a lot, but was also disappointed with the closure of some of the characters. Do I think this novel deserves a exsibilation or a standing ovation? I think somewhere in between is the best answer. I'm taking in to consideration that this book was translated from Japanese to English by Jay Rubin and Philip Gabriel, who I'm sure did a yeoman's job. Also a big factor is that in Japan this book was a trilogy. I think if you are a literary geek, you must read at least one Haruki Murakami novel and this one is a good option!ween Sakigake, Tengo and Fuka-Eri. B005I4JG80|5.0|Some people are bound to dislike this book because parenting styles can be a very touchy subject. Many people take it as a personal affront when someone advocates or uses a different method or disagrees with them. As mothers we tend to over react. This book doesn't criticize any method of parenting used by American parents, but tells us her experiences raising her children in France and her integration and embracing of many components of French parenting.<br /><br />The book is fascinating reading and I found that though I am an American, I could see the reason and the logic behind the French philosophy, a philosophy that the whole culture seems to embrace almost to a person. The idea that children need to experience controlled frustration so they learn to manage it, that they need time to be alone to know themselves and to learn to be alone comfortably, the idea of the \\"cadre\\" the structured framework of expectations and rules of the family that allow children freedom and leeway inside the boundaries set makes excellent sense.
